        function gptClean(gptText) {

                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/templates/jsp/pb2/js/plugin/thiss-ds.js" nonce="3e1144b1d62a471aac603da1b41226c9"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="3e1144b1d62a471aac603da1b41226c9">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'fb305b82-a61e-4c4b-9274-ae14c9d27f83'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

                x.parentNode.insertBefore(s, x);

                window.attachEvent('onload', riskifiedBeaconLoad)

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d3709650" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Iron sulfides with high theoretical capacity&nbsp;confront the challenges of low rate capability and severe capacity fading for sodium storage, which are mainly caused by&nbsp;poor electron/ion transport kinetics and drastic volume fluctuations during cycling. Herein, to mitigate these obstacles, a multi-step synthetic tactic involving solvothermal, carbonization, and subsequent sulfurization is put forward for the construction of wire-like structure by confining Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles in porous N-doped carbon framework (denoted as Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC) using zinc iron nitrilotriacetate as template. By partially substituting Fe<sup>3+</sup> with Zn<sup>2+</sup> in the metal coordination complex, the porous structure of coordination complex derived carbon framework can be regulated through pore structure engineering of Zn nanodroplets. The&nbsp;desired porous and robust core/shell structure can not only afford favorable&nbsp;electron/Na<sup>+</sup>&nbsp;transport paths&nbsp;and additional active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage, but also provide reinforced structural integrity of interior Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>&nbsp;particles by retarding the pulverization and buffering the mechanical stress against volume fluctuations. As&nbsp;anode&nbsp;for sodium-ion batteries, the optimal Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC delivers a high reversible capacity (743 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>&nbsp;at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>), superior rate capability (553&nbsp;mAh g<sup>−1</sup>&nbsp;at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>), and long-term cycling&nbsp;stability (602 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with 98.5% retention after 1000 cycles).</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202404767-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202404767-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The past decades have witnessed the rapid development of cost-effective and reliable energy storage systems (EESs) owing to the high rising demand for efficient utilization of renewable energy resources (i.e., solar, wind, geothermal, and biomass energy).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0001_R_d3709642e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">1</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0002_R_d3709642e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Energy Environ. Sci.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite the great success of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) in current commercialization, the issues of increasing lithium costs and uneven global distribution of rare lithium resources have largely hindered their future practical application in large-scale EESs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0003_R_d3709642e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - J. Energy Chem.">3</a>-<a href="#smll202404767-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0005_R_d3709642e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are regarded as one competitive supplement to or substitute for LIBs mainly because of the abundant, low-cost, and wide availability of sodium resources, as well as their similar working principles with LIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0006_R_d3709642e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Rare Met.">6</a>-<a href="#smll202404767-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0009_R_d3709642e500" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Sci. China Chem.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To achieve high-performance SIBs, one of the major challenges is the deficiency of more suitable electrode materials for reversible and rapid Na<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction to meet the requirements of real application.</p>
         
         <p>Among various anode materials that have been explored for SIBs, iron sulfides (i.e., FeS<sub>2</sub>, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, and Fe<sub>1–x</sub>S) have garnered considerable research attention as promising candidates, due to their low raw material cost, earth crust abundance, environmental friendliness, favorable redox reversibility, and high theoretical capacity based on multi-step conversion reaction mechanism.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0010_R_d3709642e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Nano Energy">10</a>-<a href="#smll202404767-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0013_R_d3709642e519" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano Res.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In particular, pyrrhotite Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, which possesses mixed valence states of Fe, intrinsic metallic character as well as high theoretical capacity (663&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>), has stimulated increasing attention in recent years.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0014_R_d3709642e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Small">14</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0015_R_d3709642e531" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Small">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the slow reaction kinetics arising from poor ion accessibility, and drastic volume fluctuations generated upon extended cycling, commonly induce unsatisfactory rate capability and cycling life, which severely hinders its practical utilization.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0016_R_d3709642e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Mater. Chem. Phys.">16</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0017_R_d3709642e537" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To circumvent the aforementioned issues, considerable efforts have been dedicated to improving the sodium storage properties of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> through nanostructure engineering,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0018_R_d3709642e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A">18</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0019_R_d3709642e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Small">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> heterostructure constructing,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0020_R_d3709642e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">20</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0021_R_d3709642e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nano-Micro Lett.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and integrating with carbonaceous materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0022_R_d3709642e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - ACS Sustainable Chem. Eng.">22</a>-<a href="#smll202404767-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0024_R_d3709642e559" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Adv. Mater.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, Passerini et&nbsp;al. reported the fabrication of spindle-like ultrafine Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoparticles embedded in hetero-atom (N, S and O)-doped carbon layer starting from Fe-based metal–organic frameworks (MOFs) via a reflux condensation method followed by a sulfurization process, which delivered a reversible capacity of 480 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 320 cycles at 2&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> in DEGDME electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0025_R_d3709642e573" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Small Methods">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> They also investigated the sodiation/desodiation mechanism of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> by adopting in situ X-ray diffraction (XRD) method, and revealed that intercalaction reaction took place initially before the conversion process during the sodiation process, and vice versa. Xu et&nbsp;al. reported the synthesis of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoparticles in S, N-doped carbon nanofibers through electrospinning and sulfurization processes by using α-Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> obtained from hydrothermal reaction as precursor, which could afford a stable cycling performance of 347 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 150 cycles attributing to the efficient protect of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> from structural collapse by the hetero-atom doped carbon matrix.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0026_R_d3709642e600" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Power Sources">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, Cao's group demonstrated the enhanced sodium storage properties of MOF-derived Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@C anode through modification of a ultrathin amorphous TiO<sub>2</sub> layer on the surface by using an atomic layer deposition (ALD) approach, and the TiO<sub>2</sub>-modified Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>@C composite delivered a reversible capacity of 423 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 200 cycles at 0.2 C.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0027_R_d3709642e620" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Small">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In another work, Liu et&nbsp;al. developed a multi-step method to realize the fabrication of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> nanoparticles encapsulated in N-doped hollow carbon fibers (NHCFs/Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>) by combined chemical bath deposition of Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> nanoparticles on as-synthesized hollow polypyrrole (PPy) fibers and subsequent sulfurization treatment, and the resulting NHCFs/Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> composite displayed a capacity of 517 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles and good rate capability of 444 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 20 A g<sup>−1</sup> in ether electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0028_R_d3709642e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Energy Chem.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Despite these notable advances that have been achieved, the fabrication of many previously reported Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>-based anode materials generally require complex and time-consuming procedures, and their reversibility and cycling stability of sodium storage are still unsatisfactory, especially at high rates. Therefore, it is urgently needed, yet challenging, to explore convenient protocols to obtain Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>-based anode materials with decent electrochemical performance for SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Inspired by the above mentioned strategies and discussions, we rationally designed and prepared Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles encapsulated in porous N-doped carbon framework (denoted as Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC) using bimetallic coordination complex of zinc iron nitrilotriacetate (denoted as ZnFe-NTA) as functional template precursors via a combined solvothermal, carbonization and sulfurization strategy. By adjusting the introduced amount of Zn<sup>2+</sup> in the starting materials, a set of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC hybrid composites with regulated porous structure can be obtained through the pore structure engineering of in situ formed Zn nanodroplets at high temperature. As expected, the external porous N-doped carbon framework can not only provide fast Na<sup>+</sup> transport channels and offer additional active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption and storage, but also effectively protect the interior Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles from pulverization and direct contact with electrolyte, and buffer the mechanical stress against the volume fluctuations as well. With these appealing merits, the obtained optimal Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode exhibits high reversible capacity (743 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>), exceptional rate capability (553 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> up to 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>) and long cycling life (98.5% retention after 1000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup>).</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202404767-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202404767-sec-0020-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p><b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0001">1a</a> visually describes the fabrication procedure of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 nanowires through a combined synthetic approach, which involves three major steps. First, bimetallic coordination compound of ZnFe-NTA-2 as the precursor was synthesized by a facile solvothermal reaction, in which the negatively charged amino or carboxylic groups in NTA molecules with strong metal-chelating ability could gradually react with Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Zn<sup>2+</sup> ions under moderate temperature to form unique 1D long-chain polymer structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0029_R_d3709642e730" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Phys. Chem. C">29</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0030_R_d3709642e730" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - RSC Adv.">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Subsequently, the freshly prepared ZnFe-NTA-2 nanowires were chemically transformed into intermediate Fe/PNC-2 nanowires with rich porosity during the carbonization process. The unique porous structure was attained most likely attributing to the template effect of in situ formed Zn nanodroplets at high temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0031_R_d3709642e735" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During this process, metallic Fe particles were also generated from Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions under a reductive carbothermal condition and dispersed in the PNC nanowires as produced from the decomposition of coordination polymer chains, which could result in the spatially confined growth of metallic Fe particles and restrict the agglomeration by surrounding them. Eventually, after being subjected to a gas sulfurization treatment at 500 °C under a H<sub>2</sub>/Ar atmosphere, the metallic Fe particles were completely converted into more thermodynamically stable Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles by the H<sub>2</sub>S gas, which penetrated through the external carbon layer of PNC nanowires, leading to the formation of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 hybrid composite.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202404767-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/db22a21e-1d64-4cab-81ac-d3955a708127/smll202404767-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/db22a21e-1d64-4cab-81ac-d3955a708127/smll202404767-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bda5fd85-7979-4a2a-9013-efedfb9e16d9/smll202404767-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/db22a21e-1d64-4cab-81ac-d3955a708127/smll202404767-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202404767-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202404767" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Schematic illustration of the synthetic procedure for the preparation of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 nanowires, b–d) SEM images of (b) ZnFe-NTA-2, (c,d) Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 at low- and high-magnification, respectively, and e) comparative XRD patterns of ZnFe-NTA-2, Fe/PNC-2, and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To elucidate the formation mechanism of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 nanowires, the morphological and structural transitions of the samples during the synthetic process were probed by SEM and XRD analyses. As revealed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0001">1b</a>, the typical SEM images of ZnFe-NTA-2 precursor show a uniform nanowire morphology with relatively smooth surface. These elongated ZnFe-NTA-2 nanowires exhibit an average diameter of about 240&nbsp;nm and length of several to tens of micrometers. After the sequential carbonization/sulfurization process, the 1D wire structure of ZnFe-NTA-2 is well inherited by Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 with slightly shrinked diameter to about 210&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0001">1c,d</a>). Moreover, the surface of these Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 nanowires becomes rough with abundant pores, which ensure the efficient penetration of reductive H<sub>2</sub>S gas into the interior structure for complete sulfurization. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0001">1e</a> illustrates the comparative XRD patterns of the precursor ZnFe-NTA-2, intermediate Fe/PNC-2 and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 final product. Regarding the ZnFe-NTA-2 precursor, the main diffraction peaks centered at around 12.1°, 13.4°, 16.6°, and 21.8° are most likely originating from the crystalline planes of ZnFe-NTA, which are very similar to those of previous studies about Fe-NTA in literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0032_R_d3709642e820" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Catal. Lett.">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the intermediate Fe/PNC-2, the characteristic peak located at 44.9° is well indexed to the (110) crystalline plane of cubic Fe (JCPDS No. 06–0696), while the broad peak at around 25.9° is attributed to the (002) plane of carbon with amorphous feature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0033_R_d3709642e825" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - ChemPhysChem">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> From the XRD pattern of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 final product, one can see that apart from the presence of amorphous carbon peak at 25.9°, the characteristic diffraction peak of Fe species has vanished, while a series of distinct diffraction peaks visibly emerge at 30.0°, 33.8°, 43.6°, and 53.1°, which can be well assigned to the (200), (203), (206), and (220) crystalline planes of hexagonal Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (JCPDS No. 25–0411),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0034_R_d3709642e839" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Electrochim. Acta">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> clearly demonstrating the complete conversion of metallic Fe into Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> phase within the porous carbon matrix.</p>
         
         <p>It should be mentioned that by simply altering the introduced amounts of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> in the reactions, a series of precursors and final products could be obtained under similar synthetic conditions. Therefore, the effect of introduced ZnCl<sub>2</sub> on the formation of precursors and final products was investigated by SEM characterization. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0002">2</a> displays the detailed morphologies of precursors and final products upon the introduction of different amounts of ZnCl<sub>2</sub>. From Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0002">2a–d,i</a>, one can see that the average diameter of these precursor nanowires gradually increases from 160&nbsp;nm for Fe-NTA to 200&nbsp;nm (ZnFe-NTA-1), 240&nbsp;nm (ZnFe-NTA-2) and 370&nbsp;nm (ZnFe-NTA-3), respectively. With the increasing amount of introduced ZnCl<sub>2</sub> in the reactions, it is proposed that more Zn<sup>2+</sup> ions released in the solution can partially replace the Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions in the coordination sites of Fe-NTA nanowires during the solvothermal process, thereby making 1D long-chain polymer structure more prone to break up. As a result, the coordination polymer chains of precursors become more shortened and tend to self-assemble into 1D structures with increased lateral size through van der Waals forces, thus forming nanowires with decreased aspect ratio.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0029_R_d3709642e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Phys. Chem. C">29</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0035_R_d3709642e873" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - ACS Nano">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Similarly, from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0002">2e–h,j</a>, it is observed that the variation of average diameter of these final products exhibits nearly identical trend, which increases from 130&nbsp;nm of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0 to 170&nbsp;nm (Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1), 210&nbsp;nm (Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2), and 340&nbsp;nm (Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3), respectively. The diameter shrinkage phenomenon of the final products as compared with those of precursor nanowires can be mainly ascribed to the decomposition and pyrolysis of organic coordination polymer chains during the high temperature carbonization/sulfurization process.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202404767-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7b16a285-489e-4785-9684-524e150601ac/smll202404767-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7b16a285-489e-4785-9684-524e150601ac/smll202404767-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/89a1b052-c6a3-413a-bc66-187a5925ec92/smll202404767-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7b16a285-489e-4785-9684-524e150601ac/smll202404767-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202404767-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202404767" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a–d) SEM images of Fe-NTA, ZnFe-NTA-1, ZnFe-NTA-2, and ZnFe-NTA-3, e–h) SEM images of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2, and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3, i,j) variation of average diameters for the precursor and final products obtained from the reactions with different amounts (<i>x</i>) of introduced ZnCl<sub>2</sub>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The internal structural feature of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 was further investigated by TEM and HRTEM measurement. The typical TEM image of an individual Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 nanowire segment is displayed in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0003">3a</a>, which is composed of many aggregated Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles encapsulated in a carbon shell layer with a thickness of 10–20&nbsp;nm. The HRTEM images acquired from the edge and center regions (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0003">3b,c</a>) of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 present a set of well-resolved parallel fringes with interplanar spacings of 0.265 and 0.207&nbsp;nm, corresponding to the (203) and (206) planes of hexagonal Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> (JCPDS No. 25–0411), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0036_R_d3709642e975" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Micro Nano Lett.">36</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0037_R_d3709642e975" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Molecules">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Meanwhile, from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0003">3b</a>, one can also see that the external carbon shell exhibits obvious disordered turbostratic structure with a few voids, clearly establishing the formation of amorphous and highly porous feature of carbon structure under the assistance of formed Zn nanodroplets during the high-temperature carbonization process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0038_R_d3709642e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Nano Energy">38</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0039_R_d3709642e984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The high-angle annular dark-field STEM (HAADF-STEM) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0003">3d</a>) undoubtedly verifies the typical core-shell structure of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 nanowire with distinct contrasts, and further ascertains the aggregation of interior Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles coated by a thin layer of external carbon shell. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0003">3e</a> displays the corresponding EDS elemental mapping results of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 nanowire under HAADF-STEM mode. It is clearly seen that Fe and S are primarily located in the core region, while C and N elements are detected in the whole nanowire region with high overlap, indicating the substantial incorporation of N element into the carbon matrix. The EDS spectrum as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information) reveals that the weight percentages of C, N, Fe, and S are determined to be 24.3&nbsp;wt%, 1.2&nbsp;wt%, 38.3&nbsp;wt%, and 36.2&nbsp;wt%, therefore the corresponding contents of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and PNC in the composite are estimated to be 74.5&nbsp;wt% and 25.5&nbsp;wt%, respectively.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202404767-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/7a991dc3-a32f-4407-b471-92ed288e79aa/smll202404767-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/7a991dc3-a32f-4407-b471-92ed288e79aa/smll202404767-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/be1586d4-3c2c-420b-9a6c-9ad1732d3d5f/smll202404767-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/7a991dc3-a32f-4407-b471-92ed288e79aa/smll202404767-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202404767-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202404767" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) TEM image of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2, b,c) HRTEM images of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2, d) HAADF-STEM image, e) elemental mapping analysis, f,g) N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherms, and corresponding pore volumes and average pore sizes of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2, and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3, h–k) high-resolution XPS spectra of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2: (h) Fe 2p, (i) S 2p, (j) C 1s, and (k) N 1s, respectively.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The specific surface area and pore structure properties of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2, and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3 were evaluated by N<sub>2</sub> adsorption/desorption isotherm measurements. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0003">3f</a>, all these samples exhibit an ascending trend with H3 hysteresis loops of classical type-IV isotherms, demonstrating the existence of mesopores in these samples.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0040_R_d3709642e1094" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Small">40</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> By employing the Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) multi-point method, the specific surface areas of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3 are estimated to be 12.5, 21.8, 44.3, and 65.5 m<sup>2</sup> g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. Meanwhile, the detailed pore structure characteristics of these samples were analyzed by using a Barrett–Joyner–Halenda (BJH) method. In all cases, it is observed that the samples exhibit pore sizes in the range of 2–50&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>¸ Supporting Information), further confirming their mesoporous feature. In addition, with the increase of introduced ZnCl<sub>2</sub> amount in the reactions, the overall pore volume of these samples significantly increases from 0.036 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> for Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0 to 0.068 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1), 0.159 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2), and 0.305 cm<sup>3</sup> g<sup>−1</sup> (Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3), respectively, which are probably associated with the increased average pore size from 9.6 to 15.8&nbsp;nm as ascertained in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0003">3g</a>. To better understand the effect of Zn<sup>2+</sup> concentrations on the formation of porous structure in the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC hybrid composite, we carried out additional EDS measurements on the samples of ZnFe-NTA precursor, intermediate Fe/PNC and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC hybrid composite when different amounts of ZnCl<sub>2</sub> (0.2, 0.4, and 0.6&nbsp;mmol) were introduced in the starting materials. For the ZnFe-NTA precursors as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information), we can see that the atomic ratios of Zn/(Zn+Fe) are estimated to be 2.5 at.%, 3.3 at.%, and 5.3 at.%, respectively, which are much lower than the corresponding feeding atomic ratios (9.1 at.%, 16.7 at.%, and 23.1 at.%) of Zn/(Zn+Fe) in the starting materials. This result implies that the NTA molecules tend to react with Fe<sup>3+</sup> first and it seems more difficult for Zn<sup>2+</sup> to be incorporated into the ZnFe-NTA complex as compared with Fe<sup>3+</sup>. Then, after the carbonization process, no signals of Zn species can be detected by EDS measurement from the intermediate Fe/PNC, implying the complete evaporation of Zn species and creation of rich porous structure in the derived N-doped carbon framework, which are well retained by the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC hybrid composite as ascertained by the BET results. More importantly, it is revealed that although the atomic ratios of Zn/(Zn+Fe) in the ZnFe-NTA precursor are much lower than those in the starting materials, the internal porous structure in the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC hybrid composite can be greatly affected through the pore structure engineering. From the above results, we can conclude that the amount of introduced ZnCl<sub>2</sub> in the reactions is an important parameter to regulate the internal pore structure of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC hybrid composite to obtain high specific surface area with large overall pore volume and average pore size.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0031_R_d3709642e1202" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The surface chemical compositions and valence states of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 were explored by XPS analysis. The XPS survey spectrum as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) clearly corroborates the existence of Fe, S, C, and N elements in the composite. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0003">3h</a> presents the high-resolution Fe 2p spectrum, which can be deconvoluted into two groups of characteristic peaks with binding energies of 710.1/712.3/719.4 and 723.6/726.0/732.1&nbsp;eV, assigning to Fe 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Fe 2p<sub>1/2</sub> occupying the mixed valence states of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0041_R_d3709642e1228" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - ACS Nano">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the basis of the integrated areas for these peaks, the atomic ratio of Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> can be estimated to be 0.43, which is nearly close to the stoichiometric value for bulk Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. In the S 2p high-resolution spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0003">3i</a>), the two distinct peaks at 161.1 and 162.1&nbsp;eV are ascribed to S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and S 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of S<sup>2−</sup>, respectively, while the shoulder peak appearing at 163.9&nbsp;eV is correlated to the covalent C─S bond, suggesting the existence of obvious chemical interactions between the carbon matrix and metal sulfide particles in the composite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0042_R_d3709642e1252" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, another broad distinct peak observed at 168.0&nbsp;eV can be attributed to the formation of SO<i><sub>x</sub></i> species on the surface due to the partial oxidation of metal sulfide under atmosphere condition.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0043_R_d3709642e1260" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Regarding the C 1s high-resolution spectrum as depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0003">3j</a>, four fitted peaks at around 284.3, 285.0, 286.4, and 288.1&nbsp;eV are visible, which are attributed to the sp<sup>2</sup> C─C/C═C, C─N, C─S, and O─C═O bonds in sequence, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0036_R_d3709642e1271" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Micro Nano Lett.">36</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0042_R_d3709642e1271" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">42</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0044_R_d3709642e1271" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Part. Part. Syst. Charact.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For the high-resolution spectrum of N 1s (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0003">3k</a>), three different nitrogen species can be identified at binding energies at around 398.0, 399.8, and 400.2&nbsp;eV, corresponding well to the pyridinic N (sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized N atoms bonded with two sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized carbon neighbors), pyrrolic N (N atoms in five-membered heterocyclic ring) and graphitic N (sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized N atoms with three sp<sup>2</sup> hybridized carbon), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0045_R_d3709642e1288" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Small">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This result clearly indicates the successful incorporation of N atoms into the carbon matrix, arising from the pyrolysis of NTA molecules at high-temperature. Moreover, by calculating from the integrated areas of the fitted N peaks, the detailed atomic contents of pyridinic N, pyrrolic N and graphitic N are determined to be 38.0, 38.4, and 23.6 at.%, respectively. As we know, the relatively high contents (76.4 at.%) of total pyridinic and pyrrolic N species can realize a more larger carbon interlayer distance facilitating the insertion of more Na<sup>+</sup>, create a large amount of defects as active sites, and intensify the kinetics process, which are highly beneficial for the adsorption of Na<sup>+</sup> and improvement of Na<sup>+</sup> storage capacity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0046_R_d3709642e1300" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Mater. Today">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The sodium storage behaviors of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 were first investigated in standard coin-type half-cell configuration by employing cyclic voltammetry (CV) measurement. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0004">4a</a> shows the CV curves of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode for the first five cycles at a scan rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> between 0.01 and 3.0&nbsp;V (versus Na<sup>+</sup>/Na). During the first cathodic scan, a prominent reduction peak is detected at 0.88&nbsp;V, ascribed to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and the possible formation of solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the surface of electrode.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0047_R_d3709642e1333" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">47</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0048_R_d3709642e1333" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> We conducted additional TEM characterization of the electrode as disassembled from the half-cell at an applied discharge voltage of 0.8&nbsp;V for SEI analysis, however we failed to capture meaningful TEM images to precisely reveal the SEI formation possibly due to the sensitivity of SEI film to environment and radiation damage by TEM electron beams. Meanwhile, the wide and continuous reduction peaks centered at around 0.54 and 0.26&nbsp;V are probably related to conversion reactions to form Na<sub>2</sub>S and metallic Fe. In the corresponding anodic scan, a series of oxidation peaks are observed at 1.36, 1.81, 2.11, and 2.50&nbsp;V, which are assigned to the multistep desodiation process recovering from Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0028_R_d3709642e1342" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Energy Chem.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Additionally, one pair of redox peaks at 0.03/0.09&nbsp;V are also identified, which are attributed to the insertion/extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> into/from the carbon matrix in ether-based electrolyte.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0037_R_d3709642e1350" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Molecules">37</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0049_R_d3709642e1350" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Energy Environ. Sci.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the following scans, the three reduction peaks at 0.88, 0.54, and 0.26&nbsp;V rapidly shift to 0.92, 0.60, and 0.33&nbsp;V. It should be mentioned that from the second scan onward, all the CV profiles are nearly overlapped with each other, indicating the high reversibility and stability during repeated sodiation/desodiation process. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0004">4b</a> shows the GCD profiles of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 for the first five cycles recorded at a current density of 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, which delivers initial discharge and charge capacities of 883/733 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> corresponding to an initial Coulombic efficiency (ICE) of 82%. The distinct voltage platforms in the GCD curves are in good accordance with the CV peaks, as further supported by the corresponding profiles by plotting dQ/dV versus V in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). It should be pointed out that the anomalously high sodium storage capacity of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 beyond its theoretical value is probably associated with the space charge mechanism. Similar phenomenon has also been reported in other transition metal-based electrodes in SIBs and LIBs in literature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0050_R_d3709642e1375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Nat. Mater.">50</a>-<a href="#smll202404767-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0052_R_d3709642e1375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Energy Storage Mater.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The extra capacity is most likely attributed to the contribution of surface capacitance of reduced metal nanoparticles with stored spin-polarized electrons that are formed during discharge at low potentials. Moreover, we suggest that the high ICE of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 is probably related to the unique structure of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles embedded in external porous N-doped carbon framework with limited unwanted and irreversible side reactions and decomposition/absorption of electrolyte by the host materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0053_R_d3709642e1389" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Energy Storage Mater.">53</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0054_R_d3709642e1389" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Nano Energy">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0004">4c</a> and Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information), the cycling performances of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2, and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3 and PNC electrodes at 1 A g<sup>−1</sup> were further investigated and compared. For the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode, the initial discharge and charge capacities are 881 and 734 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, corresponding to an ICE of 83%. After two cycles, a nearly 100% value of CE is obtained, reflecting the rapid stabilization and passivation of the electrode/electrolyte interface of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2. Meanwhile, after 100 cycles, the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode retains a reversible capacity of 686 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a high capacity retention of 94% with respect to the value of the second cycle, showing the higher capacity and cycling stability than those of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0 (466 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a retention of 73%), Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1 (421 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a retention of 84%) and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3 (630 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a retention of 69%) and PNC (106 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a retention of 76%). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0004">4d</a> compares the rate capabilities of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3 electrodes tested at various current densities ranging from 0.1 to 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> with 10 cycles performed for each step. It is found that the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0 electrode shows relatively low reversible capacities decreasing from 490 at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> to 216 at 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a retention of 44.0%, while the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1 electrode delivers reversible capacities from 691 to 424 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> under a current density increasing from 0.1 to 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a retention of 61.4%. Besides, the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3 electrode exhibits higher reversible capacities at low current densities from 0.1 to 2 A g<sup>−1</sup> and much faster capacity drop especially at high current densities of 5 and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> with a retention of 29.8%. In sharp contrast, the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode shows much superior rate capability up to 10 A g<sup>−1</sup> with average reversible capacities of 743, 712, 694, 678, 647, 601, and 553 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at stepwise current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, revealing a retention of 74.4% with 100-fold increment of current density. Moreover, when the current density comes back to 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, a considerably high reversible capacity of 729 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> can still be retained by Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2, indicating its better recovery of battery performance during cycling. Such outstanding rate capability of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode outperforms those of many previously reported Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>-based anode materials for SIBs (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0004">4e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0040_R_d3709642e1539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Small">40</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0055_R_d3709642e1539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Mater. Chem. A">55</a>-<a href="#smll202404767-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0060_R_d3709642e1539" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J. Alloys Compd.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202404767-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1e9a2a8f-b277-482c-9147-04bd8e43832e/smll202404767-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1e9a2a8f-b277-482c-9147-04bd8e43832e/smll202404767-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d71f7254-0d53-406c-8b7f-028a5a2fd771/smll202404767-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1e9a2a8f-b277-482c-9147-04bd8e43832e/smll202404767-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202404767-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202404767" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV curves of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>, b) GCD curves of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, c,d) comparison of rate capabilities and cycling stabilities of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2, and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3, e) comparison of rate capability with previously reported Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>-based electrodes for SIBs, f) Nyquist plots of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1, Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2, and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3 at OCV state, g) long-term cycling performance of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 at 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> with inserted cross-sectional SEM images of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode showing the variation of average thickness before cycling and after 1000 cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To better understand the enhanced rate and cycling performances of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2, electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) technique was adopted to unveil the reaction kinetics. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0004">4f</a>, all the Nyquist plots of EIS spectra present a semicircle and a straight sloped line in high- and low-frequency region, respectively, corresponding to the charge-transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) at interface of electrode/electrolyte and Warburg impedance (<i>Z</i><sub>w</sub>) of Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion within the electrode materials. According to the equivalent circuit model in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information), the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode manifests a lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of 21.8 Ω, as compared with that of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0 (47.4 Ω), Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1 (38.1 Ω) and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3 (28.2 Ω), reflecting the appropriate pore structure of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 with enhanced interfacial charge-transfer kinetics. Meanwhile, the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/0851846f-8384-4fad-a9da-68dce6a461c3/smll202404767-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202404767:smll202404767-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/smll202404767-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi>D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{D}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>) of the electrodes can be estimated from the linear region of the Nyquist plots by plotting <i>Z</i>’ versus <i>ω</i><sup>−1/2</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The calculated <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/908cf49f-092c-40ee-abab-ed39031ab325/smll202404767-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202404767:smll202404767-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/smll202404767-math-0002.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi>D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{D}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 (1.0 × 10<sup>−14</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> S<sup>−1</sup>) is somehow higher than those of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0 (1.7 × 10<sup>−15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> S<sup>−1</sup>), Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1 (4.3 × 10<sup>−15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> S<sup>−1</sup>) and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3 (5.2 × 10<sup>−15</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> S<sup>−1</sup>), indicating that the appropriate pore structure of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 can supply richer diffusion paths for electrolyte and facilitate the rapid penetration of Na<sup>+</sup> within the active material.</p>
         
         <p>To further address the long-term cycling stability of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2, GCD tests were carried out at a high current density of 5 A g<sup>−1</sup> without initial activation, as shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0004">4g</a>. Notably, the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode delivers initial discharge/charge capacities of 749 and 624 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with a high ICE of 83.3%, and retains a highly reversible capacity of 604 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles with a capacity retention of 98.5% relative to the value of the second cycle, indicating the robust structural stability upon cycling. Meanwhile, the capacity of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 decays in the period of first 250 cycles arising from the structural degradation of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> under repeated sodiation/desodiation, and then gradually increases during subsequent cycles attributing to the slow activation process with newly exposed interfaces and active sites,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0061_R_d3709642e1792" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Chem. Rev.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> while the CE maintains nearly 100% from the second cycle onward, suggesting the rapid formation of stable SEI on the surface of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2. The top-view SEM images of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode before cycling and after 1000 cycles are provided in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), from which we can see that the overall morphology of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 nanowires in the electrode is somehow maintained without severe pulverization and degradation. Additionally, post-mortem SEM characterization was also employed to investigate the change of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode during the cycling process. The detailed cross-sectional SEM images of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode before cycling and after 1000 cycles are presented in the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0004">4g</a>. It is found that the average thickness of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode only increases by ≈11.6% (from ≈69 to ≈77&nbsp;µm) at a fully discharged state after 1000 cycles, further validating the superb cycling stability of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode. It is suggested that the alleviated volume variation of active materials during cycling is probably attributed to the delicately regulated porous structure and carbon shell with enhanced mechanical buffering ability.</p>
         
         <p>Based on the above results, it is inferred that the internal pore structure of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC hybrid composite can be regulated by adjusting the introduced amount of Zn<sup>2+</sup> in the starting materials, which can greatly affect the electron/ion transport kinetics, thereby affecting the final sodium storage properties of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC hybrid composite. In detail, the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode with larger specific surface area, overall pore volume and average pore size can provide more fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion channels and offer additional active sites for Na<sup>+</sup> storage as compared with Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-0 and Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-1, thereby contributing to the higher reversible capacity and superb rate capability for Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2. However, if excessive porous structures are introduced within the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3 electrode through the pore structure engineering, the fragile structure for the active materials of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3 electrode may collapse during the battery cycling process and show poor cycling stability and high-rate tolerance, causing the obvious capacity decay especially at high rates. This conclusion is well supported by the EIS result of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 exhibiting much lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and higher <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a70bc756-f8ee-402a-9a61-353164f3c91d/smll202404767-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202404767:smll202404767-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/smll202404767-math-0003.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi>D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{D}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> as compared with those of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3, although Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-3 possesses much larger specific surface area, overall pore volume and average pore size. Therefore, from the viewpoint of achieving decent cycling and rate performance, there exists an appropriate internal pore structure of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC hybrid composite with enhanced electron/ion transport kinetics, which can be realized by introducing suitable amount of Zn<sup>2+</sup> in the starting materials.</p>
         
         <p>The reaction mechanism of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 was studied by ex situ XRD measurement performed at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>. The Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 electrode was first discharged from the open circuit voltage (OCV) to 0.01&nbsp;V and then charged back to 3.0&nbsp;V. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0005">5a,b</a> shows the ex situ XRD colored contour plots and detailed XRD patterns recorded at different sodiation and desodiation states during the first cycle against the corresponding GCD profile. Initially, at the pristine OCV state, four prominent diffraction peaks situated at 29.9°, 33.8°, 43.6°, and 53.0° are observed, which are assigned to the (200), (203), (206), and (220) planes of hexagonal Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> phase (JCPDS No. 25–0411), respectively. Upon discharging to 1.1&nbsp;V, all these characteristic peaks of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> slightly shift to low 2θ angles with 0.1° and the signals gradually weaken during this desodiation process. This indicates an expansion of lattice distances in crystal structure as a result of Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation into the host material of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>. Meanwhile, the appearance of two peaks centered at around 30.1° and 35.5° are indexed to the (22-1) and (32-1) planes of monoclinic NaFeS<sub>2</sub> (JCPDS No. 34–0935) as previously reported in literature, signifying the initial formation of intermediate phase NaFeS<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0062_R_d3709642e1955" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - Energy Environ. Sci.">62</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0063_R_d3709642e1955" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - J. Mater. Chem. A">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When the voltage decreases to 0.7&nbsp;V, the diffraction peaks of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> at 33.7°, 43.5°, and 52.9° fade away while the peaks of NaFeS<sub>2</sub> become much more pronounced, indicating the further conversion reaction between Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup> to form NaFeS<sub>2</sub>. As the discharge proceeds to 0.3 and 0.01&nbsp;V, the two peaks of NaFeS<sub>2</sub> slowly disappear during the sodiation process, while two new peaks emerge at around 38.8° and 42.6° ascribed to the (220) plane of cubic Na<sub>2</sub>S (JCPDS No. 23–0441) and the (100) plane of metallic Fe (JCPDS No. 34–0529), respectively, suggesting that the complete conversion reaction from NaFeS<sub>2</sub> to Na<sub>2</sub>S and Fe has occurred.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0064_R_d3709642e1984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Small">64</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0065_R_d3709642e1984" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Sci. China Chem.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the subsequent desodiation process, the peaks of Na<sub>2</sub>S and metallic Fe gradually become weak and disappear, while the characteristic peaks of NaFeS<sub>2</sub> situated at 30.1° and 35.5° reappear at 1.1&nbsp;V, confirming the reverse conversion reaction from Na<sub>2</sub>S and metallic Fe to regenerate intermediate phase NaFeS<sub>2</sub>. After being charged to 1.5&nbsp;V, the minor peak of NaFeS<sub>2</sub> vanishes, indicating the extraction of Na<sup>+</sup> from NaFeS<sub>2</sub>. It should be mentioned that no obvious peaks of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> can be identified at a fully charged state of 3.0&nbsp;V, which is probably attributed to the poor crystallinity feature of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> particles upon desodiation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0025_R_d3709642e2013" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Small Methods">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202404767-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/366e8ad3-a720-4854-8b15-5709130aaad7/smll202404767-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/366e8ad3-a720-4854-8b15-5709130aaad7/smll202404767-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/61f911c1-7c4b-4823-8fd0-615b594980b3/smll202404767-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/366e8ad3-a720-4854-8b15-5709130aaad7/smll202404767-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202404767-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202404767" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Ex situ XRD contour plots of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 and corresponding GCD curve during the first cycle at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup>, b) ex situ XRD patterns of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2 at different voltage states, c) GITT curve and EIS spectra at different voltage states, and d) variation of <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> and <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/3c18334b-7c85-4a3a-9b9c-02f686425867/smll202404767-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202404767:smll202404767-math-0004" display="inline" location="graphic/smll202404767-math-0004.png">
                        <semantics>
                           <msub>
                              <mi>D</mi>
                              <mrow>
                                 <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                                 <msup>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                                    <mo>+</mo>
                                 </msup>
                              </mrow>
                           </msub>
                           <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{D}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
                        </semantics></math> at different voltage states.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>To gain more insight into the electrochemical reaction kinetics of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>/PNC-2, ex situ EIS, GITT and CV measurements were conducted during the sodiation/desodiation process. The detailed EIS, GITT and CV results are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202404767-fig-0005">5c,d</a>, Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> and Table&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). All the Nyquist plots of EIS spectra were acquired at different voltage states, which were fitted by using equivalent circuit models in Figure&nbsp;<a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information). During the initial discharging process, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value decreases from 17 to 16 Ω at a voltage of 1.1&nbsp;V, which is possibly attributed to the volume expansion induced by the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> into Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> with structural stress. After discharging down to 0.7&nbsp;V, the Nyquist plot can be deconvoluted into two small semicircles, relating to the interfacial resistance (<i>R</i><sub>SEI</sub>) and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0066_R_d3709642e2101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - ACS Appl. Mater. Interfaces">66</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0067_R_d3709642e2101" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - J. Mater. Chem. A">67</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> And the slight elevation in <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value to 18 Ω can be attributed to the impact of the formed SEI layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0068_R_d3709642e2110" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - Small">68</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> When being discharged deeply to 0.01&nbsp;V, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> decreases to the lowest value of 1 Ω owing to the complete formation of conductive metallic Fe, which is favorable for the electron transfer process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0069_R_d3709642e2118" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 69 - Small">69</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> During the subsequent charging process, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value gradually increases which can be attributed to the continuous consumption of metallic Fe through the reverse conversion reaction of metallic Fe and Na<sub>2</sub>S to form less conductive species. At the fully charging state of 3.0&nbsp;V, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> is close to its original value, which mainly stems from the reformation of Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>, demonstrating the good reversibility of the Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub> electrode during the sodiation/desodiaton process.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202404767-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0040_R_d3709642e2141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Small">40</a>, <a href="#smll202404767-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202404767-bib-0069_R_d3709642e2141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 69 - Small">69</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>The Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficient (<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/433bfc8c-dcfd-408c-bee5-dd45d671ffd4/smll202404767-math-0005.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202404767:smll202404767-math-0005" display="inline" location="graphic/smll202404767-math-0005.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
                     <mi>D</mi>
                     <mrow>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">N</mi>
                        <msup>
                           <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                           <mo>+</mo>
                        </msup>
                     </mrow>
                  </msub>
                  <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">${{D}_{{\mathrm{N}}{{{\mathrm{a}}}^ + }}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>) values were determined to further understand the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics from GITT curve. The initial decline in <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b3f6ca83-f12c-4c7a-9aa3-fe7512d896e4/smll202404767-math-0006.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202404767:smll202404767-math-0006" display="inline" location="graphic/smll202404767-math-0006.png">
               <semantics>
                  <msub>
